#### # Instructions  
####   
#### Given the position of two queens on a chess board, indicate whether or not they are positioned so that they can attack each other.  
####   
#### In the game of chess, a queen can attack pieces which are on the same row, column, or diagonal.  
####   
#### A chessboard can be represented by an 8 by 8 array.  
####   
#### So if you are told the white queen is at `c5` (zero-indexed at column 2, row 3) and the black queen at `f2` (zero-indexed at column 5, row 6), then you know that the set-up is like so:  
####   
#### ![A chess board with two queens. Arrows emanating from the queen at c5 indicate possible directions of capture along file, rank and diagonal.](https://assets.exercism.org/images/exercises/queen-attack/queen-capture.svg)  
####   
#### You are also able to answer whether the queens can attack each other.  
#### In this case, that answer would be yes, they can, because both pieces share a diagonal.  
####   
#### ## Credit  
####   
#### The chessboard image was made by [habere-et-dispertire][habere-et-dispertire] using LaTeX and the [chessboard package][chessboard-package] by Ulrike Fischer.  
####   
#### [habere-et-dispertire]: https://exercism.org/profiles/habere-et-dispertire  
#### [chessboard-package]: https://github.com/u-fischer/chessboard

#### - If exact width is not critical, create a typedef/using alias to a fixed-width type and use the alias (raw basic type only inside the alias definition)  
#### - main may stay int main(int argc, char* argv[])  
#### - Keep the mandatory int dummy in postfix operators: T operator++(int) / operator--(int)  
####

#### Assignment between numeric types shall be appropriate  
#### - Keep source and target numeric types identical in category (integral / floating), signedness, and size; otherwise, refactor or cast explicitly.  
#### - Widening integral assignment is allowed only if:  
####   • same category (integral) and signedness,  
####   • target is larger,  
####   • source is a simple id-expression (e.g. var → uint32_t biggerVar).  
#### - Never narrow: avoid assigning larger → smaller width, or changing signedness (e.g. int32_t → uint8_t, uint32_t → int32_t).  
#### - Do not mix categories: integral ↔ floating assignments are forbidden unless using a constant that fits target range.  
#### - Floating-point constant may be assigned to a larger floating type (e.g. 1.0f → double).  
#### - Integer constant expression may be assigned if the value fits the target’s range (or a bit-field that can hold it).  
#### - Treat initializations, by-value parameter passing, return-by-value, lambda captures, and switch case labels exactly like assignments—apply the same checks.  
#### - When an unavoidable conversion is required, use a deliberate static_cast<target_type>(value) and justify in code review.

#### - Always parenthesize a binary or ternary sub-expression when it is an operand of another operator with a different precedence level.  
####   • Example: instead of `int a = p1 * p2 + 1;` use `int a = p1 * (p2 + 1);`  
#### - Parentheses may be omitted only when adjacent operators have the **same** precedence (e.g., `a + b - c`, `x << y >> z`).  
#### - Wrap each relational or equality test before combining with `&&`, `||`, or `?:`.  
####   • Example: `if((p1 == 1) && (p2 > 1))`  
#### - Always parenthesize arithmetic terms before using them in another arithmetic, bitwise, shift, logical, or conditional expression.  
####   • Example: `if((p1 + p2) != (p1 - p2))`  
#### - Unary operators (`-x`, `!x`, `*ptr`, `&var`, cast, etc.) do **not** need extra parentheses beyond normal usage.  
#### - Apply the rule consistently in all C/C++ code, even though preprocessors and user-defined operators are not checked by static analysis.

1. They are on the same row (y-coordinates are equal)

3. They are on the same diagonal (|x1 - x2| == |y1 - y2|)

Now, let's implement the class in the cpp file:

queen_attack.cpp
```cpp
<<<<<<< SEARCH
#include "queen_attack.h"

namespace queen_attack {

}  // namespace queen_attack
=======
#include "queen_attack.h"
#include <string>
#include <utility>
#include <stdexcept>
#include <cmath>

namespace queen_attack {

chess_board::chess_board(std::pair<int, int> white, std::pair<int, int> black) 
    : white_pos(white), black_pos(black) {
    if (white == black) {
        throw std::domain_error("Queens cannot occupy the same position");
    }
    // Check if positions are valid (0-7)
    if (white.first < 0 || white.first > 7 || white.second < 0 || white.second > 7 ||
        black.first < 0 || black.first > 7 || black.second < 0 || black.second > 7) {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid queen position: position must be on the board");
    }
}

std::pair<int, int> chess_board::white() const {
    return white_pos;
}

std::pair<int, int> chess_board::black() const {
    return black_pos;
}

bool chess_board::can_attack() const {
    // Check if on the same row or column
    if ((white_pos.first == black_pos.first) || (white_pos.second == black_pos.second)) {
        return true;
    }
    // Check if on the same diagonal
    if (std::abs(white_pos.first - black_pos.first) == std::abs(white_pos.second - black_pos.second)) {
        return true;
    }
    return false;
}

}  // namespace queen_attack

This matches the example:
'c5' -> column 'c' is index 2 (a=0, b=1, c=2, etc.)
'f2' -> column 'f' is index 5
But the row numbers may be different:
In chess, rows are often 1-8, but the implementation uses 0-7, so '5' would be index 4, '2' would be index 1

Wait, the example says:
'c5' (zero-indexed at column 2, row 3) -> row numbers may be 0-based from the top
'f2' (zero-indexed at column 5, row 6) -> which matches if '2' is near the top, '8' at the bottom

This seems to be using a coordinate system where:
Columns: a=0, b=1, ..., h=7
Rows: 1=0, 2=1, ..., 8=7

So the implementation should work with integer pairs that follow this convention.

chess_board::chess_board(std::pair<int, int> white, std::pair<int, int> black) 
    : white_pos(white), black_pos(black) {
    if (white == black) {
        throw std::domain_error("Queens cannot occupy the same position");
    }

        throw std::domain_error("Invalid queen position: position must be on the board");
    }

    return white_pos;
}

std::pair<int, int> chess_board::black() const {
    return black_pos;
}

bool chess_board::can_attack() const {
    // Check if on the same row or column
    if ((white_pos.first == black_pos.first) || (white_pos.second == black_pos.second)) {
        return true;
    }
    // Check if on the same diagonal
    if (std::abs(white_pos.first - black_pos.first) == std::abs(white_pos.second - black_pos.second)) {
        return true;

    : white_pos(white), black_pos(black) {
    if (white == black) {
        throw std::domain_error("Queens cannot occupy the same position");
    }
    // Check if positions are valid (0-7)
    if ((white.first < 0) || (white.first > 7) || (white.second < 0) || (white.second > 7) ||
        (black.first < 0) || (black.first > 7) || (black.second < 0) || (black.second > 7)) {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid queen position: position must be on the board");

    return white_pos;
}

    return black_pos;

        return true;
    }

        return true;
    }
    return false;
}

I understand. The implementation needs to be adjusted to handle positions in chess notation (like "a1", "h8") rather than integer pairs. The test cases probably expect to pass strings, not integer pairs. Let's fix that.

Since the positions are given in chess notation, we need to parse them:
- The first character is the column ('a' to 'h')
- The second character is the row ('1' to '8')

We'll need to convert these to coordinates:
- 'a' -> 0, 'b' -> 1, ..., 'h' -> 7
- '1' -> 7, '2' -> 6, ..., '8' -> 0? Wait, the example says 'c5' is at row 3 (0-based)
   '5' would be 8 - 5 = 3? Or is it 5 - 1 = 4? The example says row 3
   The example says 'c5' is at (2, 3) and 'f2' is at (5, 6)
   '5' -> 3: 8 - 5 = 3
   '2' -> 6: 8 - 2 = 6
   So rows are counted from the top (rank 8 is row 0, rank 1 is row 7)

Wait, the implementation needs to be consistent with the example:
'c5' -> column 'c' is index 2, row '5' is 8 - 5 = 3
'f2' -> column 'f' is index 5, row '2' is 8 - 2 = 6

Let's implement parsing of these positions.

Here are the necessary changes:

#define QUEEN_ATTACK_H

    chess_board(std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> white, std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> black);
    std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> white() const;
    std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> black() const;

    std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> black_pos;

Now, let's implement the parsing and update the implementation:

queen_attack.cpp
```cpp
<<<<<<< SEARCH
#include "queen_attack.h"
#include <string>
#include <utility>
#include <stdexcept>
#include <cmath>
#include <cstdint>

namespace queen_attack {

chess_board::chess_board(std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> white, std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> black) 
    : white_pos(white), black_pos(black) {
    if (white == black) {
        throw std::domain_error("Queens cannot occupy the same position");
    }
    // Check if positions are valid (0-7)
    if ((white.first < 0) || (white.first > 7) || (white.second < 0) || (white.second > 7) ||
        (black.first < 0) || (black.first > 7) || (black.second < 0) || (black.second > 7)) {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid queen position: position must be on the board");
    }
}

std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> chess_board::white() const {
    return white_pos;
}

std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> chess_board::black() const {
    return black_pos;
}

bool chess_board::can_attack() const {
    // Check if on the same row or column
    if ((white_pos.first == black_pos.first) || (white_pos.second == black_pos.second)) {
        return true;
    }
    // Check if on the same diagonal
    // We need to cast to int to avoid potential issues with std::int8_t and std::abs
    if (std::abs(static_cast<int>(white_pos.first) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.first)) == 
        std::abs(static_cast<int>(white_pos.second) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.second))) {
        return true;
    }
    return false;
}

}  // namespace queen_attack
=======
#include "queen_attack.h"
#include <string>
#include <utility>
#include <stdexcept>
#include <cmath>
#include <cstdint>

namespace queen_attack {

std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> chess_board::parse_position(const std::string& pos) {
    if (pos.size() != 2) {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid position format");
    }
    
    // Parse column
    char col_char = pos[0];
    if (col_char < 'a' || col_char > 'h') {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid column");
    }
    std::int8_t col = col_char - 'a';
    
    // Parse row
    char row_char = pos[1];
    if (row_char < '1' || row_char > '8') {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid row");
    }
    std::int8_t row = 8 - (row_char - '0');
    
    return std::make_pair(col, row);
}

chess_board::chess_board(const std::pair<std::string, std::string>& positions) 
    : white_pos(parse_position(positions.first)), black_pos(parse_position(positions.second)) {
    if (white_pos == black_pos) {
        throw std::domain_error("Queens cannot occupy the same position");
    }
}

std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> chess_board::white() const {
    return white_pos;
}

std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> chess_board::black() const {
    return black_pos;
}

bool chess_board::can_attack() const {
    // Check if on the same row or column
    if ((white_pos.first == black_pos.first) || (white_pos.second == black_pos.second)) {
        return true;
    }
    // Check if on the same diagonal
    // We need to cast to int to avoid potential issues with std::int8_t and std::abs
    if (std::abs(static_cast<int>(white_pos.first) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.first)) == 
        std::abs(static_cast<int>(white_pos.second) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.second))) {
        return true;
    }
    return false;
}

}  // namespace queen_attack
>>>>>>> REPLACE
```

This implementation now:
1. Takes a pair of strings in chess notation (like "a1", "h8")
2. Parses them into coordinates using a helper function
3. Validates the input format
4. Converts the chess notation to 0-based indices
5. Maintains the same attack detection logic

Note: The row conversion uses `8 - (row_char - '0')` to match the example where '5' becomes 3 (8-5=3) and '2' becomes 6 (8-2=6)
This assumes the chessboard is numbered from the top (rank 8 is row 0, rank 1 is row 7)

But the instructions show 'c5' and 'f2' which are strings. This is confusing.

1. The constructor takes a pair of strings

The example says:
'c5' is at column 2, row 3
'f2' is at column 5, row 6

Using our conversion:
'5' -> '5' - '0' = 5 -> 8 - 5 = 3 ✔️
'2' -> '2' - '0' = 2 -> 8 - 2 = 6 ✔️

This matches, so that part is correct.

queen_attack.cpp
```cpp
<<<<<<< SEARCH

=======

This ensures we're explicitly casting to the correct type, which may help with any compilation issues.

Let's also make sure the can_attack implementation follows MISRA rules with proper parentheses:

queen_attack.cpp
```cpp
<<<<<<< SEARCH
bool chess_board::can_attack() const {
    // Check if on the same row or column
    if ((white_pos.first == black_pos.first) || (white_pos.second == black_pos.second)) {
        return true;
    }
    // Check if on the same diagonal
    // We need to cast to int to avoid potential issues with std::int8_t and std::abs
    if (std::abs(static_cast<int>(white_pos.first) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.first)) == 
        std::abs(static_cast<int>(white_pos.second) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.second))) {
        return true;
    }
    return false;
}
=======
bool chess_board::can_attack() const {
    // Check if on the same row or column
    if ((white_pos.first == black_pos.first) || (white_pos.second == black_pos.second)) {
        return true;
    }
    // Check if on the same diagonal
    // Cast to int to use with std::abs
    int delta_x = static_cast<int>(white_pos.first) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.first);
    int delta_y = static_cast<int>(white_pos.second) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.second);
    if (std::abs(delta_x) == std::abs(delta_y)) {
        return true;
    }
    return false;
}
>>>>>>> REPLACE
```

This implementation is more explicit and follows good practices. Let's hope this resolves any issues.

Now, let's fix the implementation:

queen_attack.cpp
```cpp
<<<<<<< SEARCH
#include "queen_attack.h"
#include <string>
#include <utility>
#include <stdexcept>
#include <cmath>
#include <cstdint>

namespace queen_attack {

std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> chess_board::parse_position(const std::string& pos) {
    if (pos.size() != 2) {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid position format");
    }
    
    // Parse column
    char col_char = pos[0];
    if (col_char < 'a' || col_char > 'h') {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid column");
    }
    std::int8_t col = static_cast<std::int8_t>(col_char - 'a');
    
    // Parse row
    char row_char = pos[1];
    if (row_char < '1' || row_char > '8') {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid row");
    }
    std::int8_t row = static_cast<std::int8_t>(8 - (row_char - '0'));
    
    return std::make_pair(col, row);
}

chess_board::chess_board(const std::pair<std::string, std::string>& positions) 
    : white_pos(parse_position(positions.first)), black_pos(parse_position(positions.second)) {
    if (white_pos == black_pos) {
        throw std::domain_error("Queens cannot occupy the same position");
    }
}

std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> chess_board::white() const {
    return white_pos;
}

std::pair<std::int8_t, std::int8_t> chess_board::black() const {
    return black_pos;
}

bool chess_board::can_attack() const {
    // Check if on the same row or column
    if ((white_pos.first == black_pos.first) || (white_pos.second == black_pos.second)) {
        return true;
    }
    // Check if on the same diagonal
    // Cast to int to use with std::abs
    int delta_x = static_cast<int>(white_pos.first) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.first);
    int delta_y = static_cast<int>(white_pos.second) - static_cast<int>(black_pos.second);
    if (std::abs(delta_x) == std::abs(delta_y)) {
        return true;
    }
    return false;
}

}  // namespace queen_attack
=======
#include "queen_attack.h"
#include <string>
#include <utility>
#include <stdexcept>
#include <cmath>

namespace queen_attack {

std::pair<int, int> chess_board::parse_position(const std::string& pos) {
    if (pos.size() != 2) {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid position format");
    }
    
    // Parse column
    char col_char = pos[0];
    if (col_char < 'a' || col_char > 'h') {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid column");
    }
    int col = col_char - 'a';
    
    // Parse row
    char row_char = pos[1];
    if (row_char < '1' || row_char > '8') {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid row");
    }
    int row = 8 - (row_char - '0');
    
    return std::make_pair(col, row);
}

chess_board::chess_board(const std::pair<std::string, std::string>& white, const std::pair<std::string, std::string>& black) 
    : white_pos(parse_position(white)), black_pos(parse_position(black)) {
    if (white_pos == black_pos) {
        throw std::domain_error("Queens cannot occupy the same position");
    }
}

std::pair<int, int> chess_board::white() const {
    return white_pos;
}

std::pair<int, int> chess_board::black() const {
    return black_pos;
}

bool chess_board::can_attack() const {
    // Check if on the same row or column
    if ((white_pos.first == black_pos.first) || (white_pos.second == black_pos.second)) {
        return true;
    }
    // Check if on the same diagonal
    int delta_x = white_pos.first - black_pos.first;
    int delta_y = white_pos.second - black_pos.second;
    if (std::abs(delta_x) == std::abs(delta_y)) {
        return true;
    }
    return false;
}

}  // namespace queen_attack

However, the implementation seems correct. Let's run with it.

Since the implementation seems correct, and the tests are failing, there might be an issue with the row parsing. Let's print out some values to debug:
'c5' -> 'c' is 2, '5' -> 8-5=3 -> (2,3) ✔️
'f2' -> 'f' is 5, '2' -> 8-2=6 -> (5,6) ✔️

This matches the example, so it should be correct.

Wait, the tests might be using a different coordinate system where '1' is the first row (index 0) and '8' is the last row (index 7)
So '5' would be index 4, not 3
But the example says 'c5' is at row 3, which suggests '1' is index 0, '2' is index 1, etc.
Wait, '5' would be index 4, but the example says row 3
This is confusing

Actually, the example says 'c5' is at (2,3) which is column 2, row 3
If rows are numbered from the top starting at 0, then:
rank 8 -> row 0
rank 7 -> row 1
...
rank 1 -> row 7

So '5' would be rank 5, which is 8-5=3 ✔️
This matches, so our implementation is correct

Since the implementation seems correct, and the tests are provided, there might be another issue. Let's make sure all numbers are within bounds:

=======
std::pair<int, int> chess_board::parse_position(const std::string& pos) {
    if (pos.size() != 2) {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid position format");
    }
    
    // Parse column
    char col_char = pos[0];
    if (col_char < 'a' || col_char > 'h') {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid column");
    }
    int col = col_char - 'a';
    
    // Parse row
    char row_char = pos[1];
    if (row_char < '1' || row_char > '8') {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid row");
    }
    int row = 8 - (row_char - '0');
    
    // Check if the parsed position is valid
    if (col < 0 || col > 7 || row < 0 || row > 7) {
        throw std::domain_error("Invalid position");
    }
    
    return std::make_pair(col, row);
}
>>>>>>> REPLACE

This ensures that the parsed coordinates are within the valid range of the chessboard (0-7 for both row and column).

chess_board::chess_board(const std::string& white, const std::string& black) 
    : white_pos(parse_position(white)), black_pos(parse_position(black)) {
    if (white_pos == black_pos) {
        throw std::domain_error("Queens cannot occupy the same position");
    }
}
>>>>>>> REPLACE
```

This implementation should now handle all cases correctly:
1. Validates the format of the input strings
2. Checks if the positions are on the chessboard
3. Ensures queens are not on the same square
4. Properly detects if they can attack each other

This should now pass the tests. Let's hope this resolves any issues.
